Monday, 5 June 2006.

Grand Committee

The Committee met at half-past three of the clock.

[The Deputy Chairman of Committees (BARONESS FOOKES) in the Chair.]

Ageing (S&T Report)

Lord Sutherland of Houndwood rose to move, That the Grand Committee do report to the House that it has considered the first report of the Science and Technology Committee on Ageing: Scientific Aspects.

The noble Lord said:

    "Society in the UK is changing. More people are living longer in greater prosperity"—

and in greater health than before.

    "And over the next decades the numbers over 50, over 65 and over 80 will all increase to levels never previously seen. We need a coherent strategy to manage that demographic change".

That was a quotation from Opportunity Age—Meeting the challenges of ageing in the 21st century, presented to Parliament by the Secretary of State for Work and Pensions in March 2005. Your Lordships' committee endorses that government view with enthusiasm. However, being, as we are, a sub-committee of the Committee for Science and Technology, our primary concern was to raise questions about the place of science and technology in that strategy.

As the changes in demography have increased and the number of people living much longer and comparatively healthier lives in our society has increased, so too massive advances in relevant scientific knowledge and in our technological capacity to apply that knowledge to our environment have been our inheritance and our prospect. Along with an increase in longevity has gone an increase in scientific understanding. That is our premise. The committee's report examines some of these advances and looks at the implications for both the lives of our citizens and the policies and strategies of our Government.

We applaud the wish of the Government and its Department for Work and Pensions to regard the demographic shifts in our society as a matter of opportunity rather than crisis management, and that has been spelt out in the paper to which I referred. We are reassured by the intent of the Department of Health in its appointment of Professor Ian Philp to the post of National Director for Older People's Services that this is a sign of its commitment to meeting responsibly the needs of older people. As members of the Science and Technology Select Committee of the House of Lords, we have been impressed by the commitment of the Government, through their Department of Trade and Industry, to increase expenditure on science and technology. All that is in the pursuit of the extension of our understanding of human beings and the world in which they live and in the application of those new understandings to the
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC290
 
benefit of our citizens. There—plaudits for three government departments and I have hardly started. That is quite good.

All that is good news but, apart from the fact that initiatives run the risk of ebbing away on a tide of worthiness and words, there is a fundamental point that we wish the Government to address, alongside a number of very specific recommendations that we have made. I have to say—I shall insert it here—that in the view of many researchers to whom we spoke and whose views were conveyed to us, the ebbing away on a tide of worthiness and words has happened to a number of initiatives in this area. It seemed to be a long time before the Dynamics of Ageing initiative delivered some projects that were being funded, and the Funders' Forum had to be virtually resuscitated from near death. None the less, there are good signs that these initiatives are setting out in new directions, but we will watch with great interest whether they are successful or whether they repeat the pattern of decline which seemed to many to be their fate the first time round.

Our main contention is that there is a need for a single strategy to direct expenditure on research and the applications of research in relation to the massive demographic changes already under way in our society. A series of individual initiatives, which the government departments that I mentioned have clearly set going, occasionally give the appearance of being uncorrelated initiatives. Flowing as they do from individual departments, they do not add up to a government strategy. Whatever, one asks, happened to joined-up government?

Since becoming a Member of this House, I have formed a considerable respect for the noble Lord, the Minister from the Department for Work and Pensions, who will reply to this debate. However, I hasten to point out that, before his time, his was the department which did not feel it necessary to respond to our call for evidence, yet here he is, responding to the report. There must be a message or, perhaps better, a lesson in that; it is just difficult to see what the message and the lesson are.

The reasons for our call for a cross-government strategy have to do with the size of the demographic changes which are washing through our society and many others. To illustrate this succinctly, I can reveal to noble Lords that, for each hour spent in debating this Motion before us today, statistical life expectancy in the United Kingdom will have increased by 12 minutes. Noble Lords should think of that as they sit, their minds numbed, listening to my speech. More formally, as we note in the report, life expectancy in the UK and other developed countries continues to increase by about two years per decade. I have to add, however, that it is not within the powers of either me or my committee to issue 12-minute credit vouchers to individuals at the end of each hour.

However, there are variations: this is a statistical point. Variations, including by postcode, are significant, as spelt out in chapter 2 of our report. Figures for male life expectancy in 1998 to 2000 dropped by almost six
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC291
 
years as we proceed east from Westminster on the Jubilee line. One can record that throughout the country: the variations from one postcode area to the next are significant. We need to learn why if the words "equal opportunity" are to have substance.

Equally, as the excellent report of the Select Committee on Economic Affairs makes plain, the demographic shift is proceeding at different speeds in different developed countries—but let there be no doubt that it is proceeding. This is, as they say, a global issue; it is not simply a local or a developing-countries issue. Those countries whose full developed potential is yet to be reached can begin to read the runes if they so choose. A key economic impact lies in the ratio of those in the population who are of working age to those who are not. It is difficult to imagine, for example, that farsighted planners in the People's Republic of China have not begun to consider the consequences for this ratio of increasing longevity and its interface with the one-child-per-family policy introduced some decades ago to deal with the possibilities of overpopulation. There will be a swing in China in terms of the need for people who are trained and can take part in the economic and other areas of community life. That need will be just as strong as its current need for oil and other forms of energy. We must think about this very carefully.

However, that point is for another debate. The point that we are addressing today is that such huge demographic change has consequences for the country, among others, of massive cultural, social, health and economic dimensions. It is timely to face those consequences, and not simply in terms of the costs of pensions or the provision of care, important as those are. If, as we live longer, the quality of our well-being and our health is increased, that will be good in itself. It will also be good for the economy. However, I have to say that measuring such increases, either in healthy-life expectancy or in the impact of initiatives—not least from within the Department of Health—is made difficult in the committee's view because we do not think that measures that are fully fit for purpose yet exist. If we are to have a strategy and a policy that are worth having, we need such measures, and we need common agreement on what they are.

One of those who gave us evidence, Sir John Grimley Evans, a very distinguished physician, suggested that the ideal outcome would be to live long and to die fast. Although many of us would subscribe to the sentiment so expressed, we thought that the possibilities of selective quotation and headline mischief ruled that out as a title for the report. None the less, your Lordships will get the point.

The topic that confronted us is inevitably larger than could be contained within the report of one Select Committee, or one sub-committee of a Select Committee, carried out within one parliamentary session. Hence our first task, aided by contributors to our opening seminar—and throughout by the excellent scientific adviser, Professor Tom Kirkwood, who sat with us—was to define and focus consistently upon an agenda of priority and appropriateness, and
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC292
 
that we did. There were other areas that we could have covered, and perhaps some speakers will wish to refer to these; but there were two main areas, falling under two generic headings.

The first area was the biomedical aspects of ageing. We asked ourselves what was known about that area and how we could improve, extend and apply it. The second area came under the generic heading, "Assistive technology". What are the resources from our research basis in assistive technology that could improve the quality and extent of independent living in mature years? I shall say more about the former than the latter, but that is not to underplay the importance of the latter—and other speakers may well pick that up.

The first point to make about the biomedical aspects of ageing is a negative one. I can bring your Lordships neither promise nor threat of anyone in this Room or any foreseeable generation living for 1,000 years. On the evidence that we have, which has been given to us, this is a story of science fiction or self-publicity. We did not focus on that as an issue, much as doubtless some of the red-tops might have liked. Handy as it might have been when debating planning policies or identity cards to have someone in our midst who wrote or helped to write the Domesday Book or to draft the Magna Carta, that was not the sort of future possibility that the scientists of gerontology or geriatrics can with any confidence hold out to us or to our successors.

More interestingly, we were acquainted with a central debate among scientists about whether it was helpful to presuppose that our genes were programmed in such a way that ageing was a single identifiable causal process. I—and I stress the I—would be more than unwise in present company, and because I would be very soon out of time, if I were to attempt to summarise the complexities of that debate. But no doubt it will as a debate continue to inform appropriate research.

What was clear to us on the evidence provided is that ageing is deeply shaped and may well ultimately be caused by the fact that our cells are subject throughout our lives to a series of traumas—traumas of disease, debility and injury—and not only traumas but invasive attacks. Indeed, when many years ago I tried to learn from my wife, a medical virologist, what her science could contribute to her understanding of the human condition, this one thing became plain to my scientifically unsophisticated mind. Whereas from the outside my body may appear a serene mass at peace with the world and wholly engaged on one process alone, this is a mistaken perception. My body, and doubtless yours too, is in fact a living, walking, thinking, talking and absolutely seething battlefield. That is what happens within. That impression was reinforced by all the relevant evidence that we collected; this is a complicated matter—complicated by what goes on inside us.

These battles and their incursions all take their toll, albeit at different times and at different rates for individuals. Ageing is person-specific. There is no universal and inexorable temporal process to which we
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC293
 
all conform in exactly the same way, at exactly the same speed. There are very important implications of this for our understanding of ageing and the contribution that science can make to this. One is certainly that many of the traumas to which I referred are part of the assault of diseases that are specifically associated with age. The association is, however, complex; it is not the single causal root of ageing, although the diseases in question contribute to the ageing process. However, we are undoubtedly more susceptible to their impact because of the cumulative history of ourselves to which I have already referred. These diseases—and I give some examples, although others will doubtless be mentioned in debate—include arthritis, cancer, forms of dementia, and diseases of the heart. Interestingly, some are absent, because we have dealt with them—for example, tuberculosis, which used to be a major cause of death. We can reflect on why that is no longer on the agenda. Science had something to do with it.

Our conclusions from what we learnt about this range moved through policy and strategy to specific applications. The policy issue seems obvious: the demographic change in the shape of our society requires any sensible government—and I count our Government among those—to think through the long-term consequences for what we do and how we use our human and financial resources, and to do so as a matter of urgency. This is clearly beginning to happen in areas such as pensions, employment, health and care, and we welcome it. However, of equal importance is the use of these resources in relation to scientific research and, as we shall see, to the domestic and built environment.

Our specific recommendations include the following. The first, a very high priority in the scientific area, is to provide adequate resources for appropriate longitudinal studies. That is a lesson that we learnt from our visit to the National Institute for Aging in the USA. We do not suggest setting up such a body in this country. However, one of the things that the USA can do with greater facility than us, not simply because it is a wealthy country, is to set up longitudinal studies with the financial commitment that makes that possible.

A high priority is more specific and accountable targeting of resources on the diseases that I have already mentioned which are overwhelmingly associated with ageing. That can be done by, for example, encouraging the relevant charities and setting the requirements of public sources of funding to ensure that the investigation of cancer, for example, can pay proportionate financial attention to those forms more prevalent and invasive in old age. Cancer is now researched thoroughly in many ways, and we are all delighted at that. We are calling for specific focusing on the ways in which cancer, in its forms prevalent in old age, affects the ageing process.

In this, as in the other diseases that feature in our understanding of old age, advances in science have given us the tools to make significant progress in understanding, treatment and amelioration, even if not in outright prevention. For example, the Human
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC294
 
Genome Project, the creation of UK Biobank and the inauguration of Generation Scotland provide potential for progress that is beyond the wildest dreams of our scientific predecessors. In passing, I must declare an interest in Generation Scotland, as I chair its advisory board. It is a study of, in principle, 50,000 families in Scotland, recruited through general practices, in which it is hoped to establish correlations between genetic inheritance and susceptibility to specific illnesses. Studies such as that, the information now being collated and stored in UK Biobank and the basic work of the Human Genome Project make possible scientific understanding not possible in previous generations. However, these massive developments owe much to public and charitable expenditure, and we must capitalise on that expenditure.

A particularly opportune and important development would be a strategic decision to focus proportionate resources on programmes to identify the interaction between genetic and non-genetic risk factors. The scientists who are important in this field tell us that the interaction of genetic and non-genetic risk factors is a key area because of the complexity to which I was trying to draw attention earlier. There should be a specific recommendation to involve adequate numbers of older people in relevant drug trials, simply because, as we were told by all the specialists in the field whom we met, it is a mistake to assume that results from drug trials on 45 year-olds can simply be read forward to discover what the impact of those drugs will be on 65, 75 and 85 year-olds.

I shall draw attention to one further recommendation, to which urgency has once again been lent by the National Audit Office report incorporating conclusions on the treatment of stroke. Well-established research indicates that early diagnosis and treatment of the causes of stroke is critical to the maintenance of brain function and capacity. The longer the delay, the greater the damage to the brain, the greater the impact on the life of the individual, and the more extensive and costly the process of treatment and rehabilitation.

One specific requirement is the provision of scanners and staff to service them on a round-the-clock basis at all accident and emergency centres. I give your Lordships a comparison from the National Audit Office report. In Canada, the target is to scan stroke victims within three hours; that is a realistic target for them. In the UK, if you are indicated as being in need of a scan on admission, you have a 30 per cent chance of the extended target period of 24 hours being met. That is a huge discrepancy in provision. It is not simply for the well-being of individuals, critical though that is. The cost to the system, in extended and extensive treatment and rehabilitation, is great.

There is a problem of cross-boundary and cross-departmental budgets, and who saves from one investment and gains from another. I simply ask that this be closely looked at. I shall not pursue it further, since there was an excellent debate on an Unstarred Question on this topic, inaugurated by the noble Lord,
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC295
 
Lord Rodgers of Quarry Bank, on Tuesday 23 May. However, the lesson is obvious and the recommendation clear. I am aware that, in the time I am using, I should not move into the details of assistive technology. There is a lot in the report, and I dare say it will be referred to in further debates.

Time forbids me to outline many specific recommendations in the report, but they will doubtless be highlighted by the star-spangled list of speakers to follow. Our central message is of the need for an integrated strategy which can maximise the benefit that the country's first-class science and technology base can offer to the business of dealing with these exceptionally important questions. That is the central point that we wish to push home time and again.

We endorse the need for a Minister of Cabinet rank to champion the cause of older people and the building of appropriate knowledge bases. We recommend that the lead responsibility for the co-ordination of ageing-related research be placed within the Department of Trade and Industry. We recommend and ask for an assessment of how our significant national research expenditure and its patterns meet the size of the task, and the opportunity which demographic change defines.

None the less, the Minister will be pleased to note that we have not asked for more money outright, although that would be a bonus. Rather, we ask that we spend the available money more strategically, effectively, efficiently and in ways more proportionate to the needs of our dramatically changing society. I beg to move.

Lord Turnberg: Perhaps I may begin by saying what a pleasure it was to be a member of this committee, not simply because of the interest of the subject matter but because of the superb chairmanship of the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland. I should like to recommend that we retain him as a full-time adviser to your Lordships' House.

I shall focus on the need for more and better research into ageing. The fact is that we are all living longer than ever before and, so far, there seems to be no slackening off in the rate at which we are gaining extra years of life. The slope of increasing longevity continues in a straight line upwards. Perhaps I may put a spin on what the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland, said about living 12 minutes longer for every hour. Although it may seem like that during the course of this debate, the fact is that a child born now will live on average an hour longer than one born five hours ago. So we have to be born now in order to gain.
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC296
 

The overall figure for life expectancy at birth in the UK is very good but it covers an enormously wide variation across the country. The reasons for that variability alone are certainly worthy of more research. However, the overall steady increase suggests that we must be doing at least something right in medical research, since it has been shown that about 50 per cent of the increase in longevity can be attributed to advances in medical care which themselves are the result of research. That in turn suggests that we cannot and should not slacken off. As we heard in the committee, there is considerable evidence that all was not well in our support for research into ageing. We could do much better. And it was not simply a question of more government money, although that is always welcome. It was much more a problem of a lack of an overall strategy and drive. There are many different funders of research—charities, the MRC and government—which each fund some research into ageing. However, it is poorly co-ordinated with little sense of direction so that the overall effort is inefficiently applied. Much that is done is not labelled as ageing research—for example, into stroke or cancer, which affects the old disproportionately.

We recognise too that research into ageing can be roughly divided into research into the ageing process itself—what is it that makes cells, tissues and organs grow old, and what influences can we bring to bear on those processes?—and research into the diseases that disproportionately affect the elderly. However, these two types of research are closely intertwined, because the very process of ageing makes our cells and tissues more susceptible to disease. Why that should be deserves much greater attention.

Furthermore, the ageing process does not just begin just in old age. It starts from the moment we are born, unfortunately, and continues throughout life. It has clear implications for understanding all sorts of diseases which affect all age groups, from cardiovascular disease to cancer to mental disorders. Therefore, a better understanding of the ageing process itself is fundamental not only for improvement in the health of old people but for people of every age.

So, it was with some disappointment that we read the Government's response to what we felt was a carefully reasoned and reasonable report. Of course, with the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland, in charge, how could it not be? I repeat: it was not primarily a bid for more funds. There was little in the report which should have provoked the rather defensive tone of the response. For example, because of the pervasive nature of research in this field, it crosses many boundaries and falls into the lap of a wide range of potential funders, few of whom take it as a high priority, with a few notable exceptions such as the Alzheimer's Society and the British Society for Research on Ageing. Other charities, such as the Wellcome Trust, are less directly involved, and it falls between the stools of the MRC, the ESRC and the BBSRC and, of course, the Department of Health.

While this multiplicity of potential funders may offer some advantages, these are lost because none has a prime responsibility for this research, and the
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC297
 
mechanisms that they set up to bring a degree of coherence between them have singularly failed. The Funders' Forum, which supposedly brings many of these funders round the table, has rarely met over the past few years. It has no power, nor does it have any support particularly from a specific government department. The Government's response was to suggest that it should be possible to make this forum work—and maybe it will; but no one whom we spoke to in the field believes that that is highly likely without a much more radical approach. Nor does anyone in this area of work feel that the lead department of state for research into an essentially medical and biological field should necessarily be the Department for Work and Pensions. Surely it is much more the province of the Department of Health or the Office of Science and Innovation. That is where the political will is more likely to lie.

A tremendous opportunity is presenting itself, with some far-reaching and innovative changes being made in the funding and organisation of medical research in general. The Chancellor's Budget announcement of the bringing together of MRC and NHS R&D funds so that the fruits of basic biomedical science can be transcribed seamlessly into clinical research and on into clinical practice is a very important initiative. It is just the sort of thinking that we should be applying to research into ageing. The setting up of the UK Clinical Research Collaboration is an imaginative and potentially important development and brings together many funders of clinical research under the auspices of the NHS R&D division. It, too, provides a valuable model on which we could easily build for research into ageing. It was disappointing in view of that potential to find no mention of ageing research in the recent UKCRC publication which reviewed research support in the UK, broken down into a wide range of different clinical areas. Ageing research did not figure once.

I am sure that the Department for Work and Pensions does marvellous work, but it should concentrate on what it does best. Medical research is not what it is set up to do best. What I would like to ask my noble friend the Minister, whom I know well from his previous incarnation in the Department of Health, where he was a real champion of medical research, is whether he will consider using the mechanisms now opening up and the new funding arrangements to give responsibility for developing a strategy for research into ageing to the UKCRC. It should be given a strong remit to set up a structure which will provide the necessary cohesion between the disparate parties and given targets and a clear timetable to achieve them. Clearly, it will need a strong direction from a lead department. Can I tentatively and respectfully suggest that he might care to hand that over to the Department of Health, working with the Office of Science and Innovation? I know that he will not take to that very easily—he will resist; but I hope that he can see the advantages of the suggestion, as it would send a message out to all those involved that the Government are taking the matter seriously.

Baroness Murphy: According to the American wit and philosopher, Ralph Waldo Emerson, old age,

    "brings along with its uglinesses the comfort you will soon be out of it".

He wrote that in the 1880s, when he was just in his 60s. He could not have been more wrong, of course; indeed, he soldiered on until he was over 80. But later life now goes on a great deal longer than ever before. I shall collect my bus pass next January and, if I am lucky enough to live as long as my mother, who is now 92, I shall have spent one-third of my life over pensionable age. By all accounts, it seems that I am adding another 12 minutes all the time!

I, too, pay tribute to Grimley Evans's wonderful line, "live longer, die faster". But the fact of the matter is that we do not know whether the time spent in poor health and disability, which precedes death, is diminishing or not. As others have said, we are not clear, because the necessary longitudinal research has never been done. Given the state of government interest—and I shall perhaps not be as polite about government interest in ageing issues as other noble Lords—I am not surprised that we know so little. Many of my colleagues on the Select Committee—so ably chaired by the knowledgeable noble Lord, Lord Sutherland of Houndwood—were shocked by the dismissive and poorly constructed government response to the report. Sadly, I was not surprised, having been exposed to the deeply ingrained unconscious ageism of several departments over the past 25 years as a psychiatrist gerontologist and, at one time, the Chief Medical Officer's adviser in my field. I would have been surprised if we had a different response.

The difficulty is that the aged are regarded as a separate tribe with funny habits, competing with other separate tribes for funds. They are perhaps worthy of anthropological study, but they are not you and me. This mythical tribe are dependent economically, need a lot of looking after, are senile, deaf and walk around bent with sticks. The Department for Transport has a warning triangle to be put up outside care homes and nursing homes which has two people from this funny tribe on it—thin and ancient crones with bent backs. They look like no two people any of us has ever met, but we instantly recognise the stereotype. The sad thing is that the Government are no different from the general population in their stereotypes. When I tell new medical students that the vast majority of their patients will be over 75, they look quite stunned and upset at first because they think they will be treating people like those in the lovely BUPA ads, with beds full of young women. The stigma of old age is contagious. Those who work with the very aged in health and social care realise all too quickly that the brand-new hospital building will be commandeered for the young, and the old secondary hospital without
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC299
 
an A&E department will be allocated for the old, and that it will all too quickly fill up with the slow stream of those no longer welcome on the acute hospital site.

The British Medical Journal used to publish every year a list of who got clinical distinction awards, as chosen by doctors. It was a wonderful hierarchical list of those specialities that doctors perceived as elite. Immunology and neurosurgery were at the top, learning disabilities at the bottom, and care of the elderly came just above that. The National Service Framework for Older People standard 1 states that older people will get services based on need, which is clearly designed to deplore and root out ageism. It is excellent that we have that standard, but there are many well-researched examples of where age, especially great old age, is a barrier to getting the interventions which younger people do, even in those cases where the benefit, such as cardiac pacemakers and knee replacements, may have greater economic and social benefits in the aged than in the young. When a disease is perceived as an old person's disease, such as stroke and dementia, the services available to them are likely to be much poorer than for diseases of younger people.

Compare the extraordinary difference of investment in research in cancers affecting different age groups. According to figures produced by the National Cancer Research Institute, 18 per cent of total cancer research funding is spent on the leukaemias, a group of cancers responsible for just 3 per cent of the mortality from cancer, including many younger people. A similar percentage of the total funding is spent on the combined research efforts into cancers of the lung, prostate, oesophagus, stomach, bladder and pancreas, which are responsible for the deaths of nearly 60 per cent of those who die from cancer, but mainly affecting people in their 60s, 70s and 80s. Think of the fantastic achievements in the leukaemias, of which we are so proud, and consider what we might have achieved if we had the same sort of proportionate investment in the cancers affecting older people.

These anomalies in funding policies are surely something that government are meant to rectify by taking a dispassionate—or perhaps I mean compassionate—view of the various calls on their resources and allocating according to total population benefit. I am sure that our report was calling for simply that. However, government have only perpetuated the attitudes of scientists and healthcare professionals to older people. They say all the right things in official documents but have steadfastly refused to include age equality in the provision of goods and services in equality Bills. They have recognised employment rights for older people, which have been tackled and improved, but we still have no rooting out of age discrimination in government departments.

Perhaps I may return to the report. We said that the national service framework was woolly, or words to that effect, when it could have had hard targets. I shall
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC300
 
raise the stroke issue yet again although I did so in a previous debate. It is clear that every stroke victim should be scanned within three hours, as we have heard and as happens in Canada and Australia. However, our national service framework is still couched in soft aspirations and is patronising when it could have been challenging. I hope that the new strategy for stroke services will include a measurable level, a real target that we can get to grips with.

The difficulty we have is the profound distaste and fear that the general population feel about the very aged. "Ugliness" is Emerson's word, not mine, but it is a good one to help us understand the deep stigma of great old age. That generalises to all matters of ageing, so that even though the all-encompassing issues of population ageing that have a major impact on all of us in terms of building design, purchase of goods, transport, design of clothes and telecommunications, all of which are all well identified in this report, government departments continue to shrink before the spectre of the aged, with the consequences that we all recognise here. They include the poor co-ordination of ageing research, because they have not grasped that it is about the whole population, not a subgroup; poor investment in training for building up research capacity, because there is a belief that anyone with their head screwed on would not want to do it; and a tolerance of the dearth of academic clinical gerontology in the past 15 years, because it is now up to universities to react to market forces and the RAE. It is a vicious cycle that can be broken only with real leadership and government determination.

It is not too late for the Government to grasp the issues or to understand that this is the whole nation's problem. Some 20 years ago I witnessed the relatively modest investment that the US Government made, through the National Institutes of Health, in funding clinical research fellows in geriatric psychiatry and the explosion of enthusiasm and first-class research that came about over the next decade as a result. In the UK we used to pride ourselves that our services in older people's mental health were world beating. They were world beating 20 years ago, but that progress has been buried by the United States' dramatic investments in this area and in the science of the dementias, which all started relatively modestly with 12 NIH-funded research fellowships and a co-ordinating structure. As the external advisor, I was well aware of how much could be achieved with a small input and great understanding by a government agency.

The issue of pensions has at last been grasped, possibly because of the economic impact pensions have on younger people. So, will other matters of ageing be tackled at last? This report makes modest suggestions, and government leadership of the co-ordination of existing efforts seems like a good place to start. I look forward to hearing what the Minister has to say in reply to this debate and in particular to what the Government will do to root out age discrimination in their own ranks.

research pertinent to ageing and disability, the British Society for Research on Ageing, the British Society of Gerontology and the British Geriatrics Society all said that they,

The National Institute of Aging has a well co-ordinated programme, and there is strong encouragement from federal and state government and private industry to participate in development in this field.

I have found no hotel in London that is adequately supplied for wheelchair users.

Much could be done in the built environment to facilitate a good quality of life, from simple adaptations of existing facilities to more extensive modifications. Baroness Finlay of Llandaff: Like other noble Lords who have spoken, I found it an enormous pleasure to be part of the committee chaired so ably by the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland, who I believe is going to be starting a distance learning course on how to chair committees—I say that in jest!

Some years ago in Wales, we had a health campaign, the title of which was framed by Deirdre Hine, who was then Chief Medical Officer for Wales. It was "Adding life to years and years to life". As we have heard, "years to life" is already happening; it is happening as we speak. "Life to years" is urgent, and research may take some time to come through. What emerged from our report is that some things can be done immediately. Helping people to live as well as possible for as long as possible means preventing them, by and large, ever encountering medical services and making sure that when they do, those services are central to their needs, are not patronising and recognise the intrinsic worth and value of every human being who comes through their doors, irrespective of their age and of what might appear, at first sight, to be their disability.

Those who are now in retirement are an enormous resource to our community, which is hugely undervalued. They assist as the eyes and ears of the community; they assist the police; they assist neighbours and they assist in the health and social care of those around them. That is apart from assisting as unpaid counsellors to the next generation, particularly to their grandchildren when they run into problems. How do we keep this generation active and able to contribute? It is all about activity, both physical and mental. There is a phrase, "If you do not use it, you
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC304
 
lose it" and nothing could be more true to mobility and mental agility in the elderly. The problem is that in our society the elderly have become fearful of crime, are more hesitant to go out and have become increasingly dependent on the car.

I will start by talking about driving. In the report, we recommended that the DVLA should not exclude the option of self-regulation in recertification for driving. We were far too timid. Other papers that I have found since then show that there is no evidence at all, particularly from Sweden and Australia, that the accident rate is adversely affected by people not having a medical. In fact, older drivers are the most responsible, and they take themselves off the road before they are dangerous. If they were more computer literate and more able to use computers in places such as libraries, their self-certification would become much easier, and they would not have to pay huge medical private certificate fees to have their licence renewed. The medical undertaken to renew licences does not detect the type of things that make people a danger on the road anyway, so it is an incredible waste of money.

Car design has always centred around young people. I commend Audi, because the A2 is remarkably easy for older people to get in and out of because it is higher off the ground. The dashboard has the equivalent of a grab rail, and the seats are the right distance from the door. They market it to the young, but I am sure that it has been designed for the old. I only wish that other car manufacturers would do the same.

For older people to go out and about and feel safe, another part of technology that they are often dependent on is their mobile phone. Many older people use heavy, old phones. The reasons are simple: the display is larger and the keypad is easier to operate. New mobile phones are designed by young men who want to play games on them, by and large, or to send flirtatious text messages or videos to other young people. But old people are the main users of the telephone for security and communication. I do not understand why telephone companies have not seen this as a phenomenal marketing opportunity. A further problem is that many people who are on their own are telephone-dependent in order to contact friends and relatives. However, the cost of a phone call during the day is astronomically high, and many old people are not aware of the very cheap ways in which they can make calls. The marketing of cheap calls does not target them specifically and is not written in a way that is particularly helpful.

Another huge marketing opportunity is in technology around the house to help people stay in their own home longer. An example is the washing machine. My mother does not want to change her washing machine because she is frightened that a "fancy" one will boil her woollies. I do not blame her because a new washing machine did that to mine. My mother wants a reliable and simple washing machine which will wash her sheets at a high temperature and her woollies at a low temperature. The same goes for other items, such as the DVD player. Having joined Blockbusters, which I always thought was for the
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC305
 
younger generation, my mother was dismayed to find that although she can get DVDs and watch interesting films, the player is so complicated that she had to make a phone call to a relative in France to be able to watch a film. Although that sounds trivial, it is a big issue that is being raised by lots of older people. On a microwave, they want to turn the dial and see the number of minutes. They need to be able to read the typeface above the buttons clearly and easily. They are not worried if it does not do 101 things.

The same goes for IT. In the Government's response, they highlighted that 78 per cent of people over the age of 65 have never used a computer, compared with 35 per cent of the population at large. Why? It is frightening and complex, and it may crash. Older people worry about viruses and access to their credit cards should they shop on the internet. There is a huge marketing opportunity, and I do not understand why Apple has not picked up on it. It claims that it can supply computers for £100 for Africa. Older people want a simple framework, which, in fact, most of the population want. They want to word process, search the internet and send e-mails. I know 80 year-olds who are even going to give PowerPoint presentations to their University of the Third Age class. That is an added extra, but they will want to do a fairly simple PowerPoint presentation. Then it does not become frightening, particularly on a computer that automatically updates its anti-virus software.

Shopping on the internet is another enormous tool available to older people, but it is under-marketed to the age group that would benefit most. Occasionally, things go wrong. My mother-in-law ordered 11 packs of ham instead of one. So every visitor for a week was sent away with a pack of sliced ham. But never mind, it was quite amusing. However, that type of shopping has revolutionised the lives of people who are much more housebound, cannot carry bags, are worried about being mugged when going to the shops and have difficulty walking.

In my final couple of minutes, I shall cover difficulty in walking and preventable problems. I started by saying that we want to keep people out of healthcare. Falls are the fifth most common cause of death in the elderly. They are very simple and are probably massively under-reported. Mobility programmes have shown that the instance of falls can be decreased. The Nijmegen programme has cut falls by 46 per cent. That is a dramatic response. People are walking more steadily after a very simple input. Drugs are one of the biggest contributors to falls, and it is well recognised that patients on four or more drugs are very much more likely to fall. Although it is not proven, that is probably a drug side-effect, because the bodies of the elderly handle drugs differently. Their first-past metabolism is a little different. A young person may absorb 5 per cent of a drug. If that were increased to 10 per cent, the dose absorbed could be doubled from 5 milligrams to 10 milligrams. If the trials were done with a young person where the expectation was that 5 milligrams of a 100 milligram tablet would be absorbed, the chances are that an older person would certainly keel over on such a dose. Drug destruction is
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC306
 
different, elimination is slowed a little and renal function is not so good. It also seems that tissue sensitivity alters. So we need to have research into all aspects of ageing. Such research could be done quite quickly and yield results.

I return to the issue of the internet and the ability of the elderly to search. They want to know about the drugs that they are on and the illnesses they have. If they can access the internet, they themselves can search for drug interactions and side effects. They will be more likely to spot what is going on and more empowered to take control of their own healthcare, and will thereby be more able to maintain their independence. If we do not maintain their independence, we will not only incur enormous expenditure; we will lose an enormous resource in our communities.

After rereading the report, I, like the noble Lord, Lord Soulsby, have come to the view that we need something along the lines of a national institute. However, it must go way beyond health and look at all the different aspects of assisted technology.

Baroness Greengross: I congratulate the committee and the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland, on the way in which he chaired it. It is an excellent report. I am not surprised at the Government's less than enthusiastic response, as this is not the first very positive report to have met such a response over the years. It has not always been this Government but this is always the sort of response we get on matters to do with ageing. I am thinking in particular of the Foresight initiative. It made some important and good recommendations and received a similar response. The noble Baroness, Lady Murphy, is right. A lot of this is due to conscious and unconscious ageism which is shared among the population but often not recognised by anyone. We know about clinical trials excluding older people. We know about denial of access to certain treatments. We have heard about the awful situation regarding stroke. We have had a quotation from Professor Grimley Evans. I remember him saying once that if we are to deny heroic surgery to people on the grounds of age, as often happens, and a belief that it is not worth it, we should remember that the very old often survive such surgery better than young people because they are pretty tough to have survived to that age. So their record of recovery is actually very good.

I would like to concentrate on technology in this brief speech and discuss some of the recommendations in the recent OECD report, which said that technology offers tremendous scope for promoting health, functional ability and independence for everyone. I stress that technology offers that to everyone and not only to older people. That certainly applies to people with disabilities. Their needs are not all the same but are very much interrelated. As has often been said, if we get it right for the older generation we will get it right for everyone.

We know that in a medical and care context much more research is needed into which technologies are appropriate and effective. Some technologies are
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC307
 
underused. We know that costly equipment is often used only during certain hours of the day, which is not the case in many other countries. We know that there are other reasons why treatment is different for some groups. One outrageous example is the cardiac care given to women compared with that given to men. We also know that some high-tech equipment is too routinely used. Despite increasing concern about the rising costs of long-term care, there is remarkably little research into the issue of how technology can help and, compared with clinical settings, little evaluation of the existing technologies. We also need a true cost/benefit analysis. Such an analysis must be done over a longer period and looked at properly.

As we know, we need to move from a disease control model to a quality of life model. There is a lot of investment in clinical technology but relatively little in that which can assist with ageing and impaired mobility. I say that as patron of two disability organisations and not just in relation to ageing. High-tech medicine is usually associated with saving life in acute and trauma situations and is not associated sufficiently with improving quality of life and functional ability and thus reducing health and care costs, especially as regards people in their own homes who suffer from chronic conditions, including older people.

One result of the success in controlling formerly fatal disease is ironically, as we know, greater long-term dependency of people with a range of conditions that require support through care and technology, which are closely interlinked. We know that there are all sorts of ways of doing that. Last week I was in Denmark at a global conference on ageing. There were world experts there in policy, practice and research in ageing, including Professor Tom Kirkwood, whom I know very well. On display was a beautiful, two-foot long, lovely, cuddly seal called Paro, which can respond to seven languages, open and shut his eyes, move, be cuddled and cuddle back. He is a Japanese robot which can do all sorts of things. I was as sceptical as anyone else about the use of robotic pets, but I saw all the experts walk past the counter where Paro was on display and pick him up and hug him. The initial results of the research into how he is used show that people of all ages suffering from insomnia, stress-related illnesses, mental illness, depression and a lot of other diseases, including dementias, are getting very good results by cuddling Paro, with his gorgeous eyes, talking to him and receiving his response. I was impressed by that robot, which I did not think I ever would be.

Assistive technology should be combined with universal design and should not always be designed just for older people or with older people in mind. Such technology will always be necessary in many contexts but, because of the changing demography and the disposable incomes of older people, more conclusive design across a whole range of products is going to be essential. I do not understand why we always specialise in designs that are good for older people. I disagree a little with my noble friend Lady Finlay in that the
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC308
 
marketing of such products often has the older population in mind. For example, the big-handled kitchen utensils are designed for people who have a problem with gripping utensils that are too narrow, and they are very good for people with disabilities of all ages. In the wider design of housing and kitchens, amazing results can be achieved by raising cupboards so that young pregnant women do not have to scrabble about on the floor to see what is on the shelves in the kitchen. Such design is not just for older people: we have to look at all the uses of assistive technology for those with varied needs.

With regard to safety and security in the home, we know that the third and fourth generation of technological systems are extremely discreet and well designed and can monitor many situations in the home, and that can help huge numbers of people to maintain autonomy. We know that there can be great support for those suffering from memory loss and dementia and that that can reduce social isolation. We also know that very simple messages—through Skype, for example—can keep people in touch, viewing and talking to relatives across the world. It does not cost anything; it just needs to be set up.

We also know of online shopping, entertainment and leisure. The interactive pursuit of education that keeps people's minds active has been mentioned. There are also new ways of working involving ergonomics in the workplace. We know that technology is important in that. Experiments done in Scandinavia some time ago made enormous changes to the design of hospital settings and the manufacture of buses and coaches. By changing the way people work, older workers could stay in work much longer because they had a complete product to work on, rather than working on an assembly line. We must look at such things in more detail. We also know that it is important to maintain autonomy in the workplace for workers. Michael Marmot's studies, among others, have shown that although we have increased longevity, inequalities in longevity and life expectancy can be reduced significantly by changing the way people work, so that they have increased self-determination, which gives them much better life expectancy, as well as a better quality of life.

We know that new technology is not always taken up by the older population. I agree with what my colleagues said. But there are some good examples, and they need more support from the Government. Age Concern and Microsoft have set up a huge number of silver surfers' centres around the country, which get a lot of people interested. People are learning, and what they learn on must be simple and well designed. If people cannot cope with something, whether a washing machine, a computer or a telephone, that is not the fault of the consumer, but of the designers, because design has to be made for the consumers for whom it is intended. It is important that designers grasp that. However, many marketing companies have recognised the need for marketing to the ageing population. If one looks at the shape of cars today, with high roofs so that one can sit up, flexible doors and large dashboard notices, not just Audi but
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC309
 
most car manufacturers have changed the shape of cars. They do not market them for older people, but they are the people who buy them.

However, things are changing. There are some good examples: the noble Lord, Lord Sutherland, will know the Utopia project run by four Scottish universities, which looks at how the needs, desires and abilities of older people can be taken account of in design. It is an excellent example. There is a lovely example in New South Wales that shows how IT can build a virtual community of those with an interest in the use of technology for the benefit of older people, not just the end users, but the health and education sectors and governments.

I agree with noble Lords and with organisations including Help the Aged who say that the Government must give ageing a greater priority. It is a huge issue. There must be more funding for research and appropriate leadership from a prominent department of state. Indeed, a Minister with specific responsibility would be an appropriate appointment that would demonstrate the Government's commitment to meet the challenges and to benefit from the opportunities that the new longevity presents to us all.

Finally, I have a brief description of CAST, which was established in the United States in 2003. It is a national coalition of 400-plus technical companies, ageing service organisations, university research departments and government representatives. I have two or three copies of the document and, if the Minister would like to look at it, I would be very happy. It is an example of another way of coordinating the work. If we do not go with the national institute model, we could look at something like it.

Lord May of Oxford: First, I echo the pleasure of participating in the Select Committee inquiry and express my admiration for the chairmanship of my noble friend Lord Sutherland. However, I must also mention my counterbalancing disappointment in the Government's response to the results of our labours.

In the spirit of an earlier Office of Science and Technology initiative of the late 1990s on extended quality of life, elegantly called EQUAL, the report recognised that people are living longer. It went on, after extensive consultation and discussion, to recommend ways of ensuring that these longer lives are lived in good health, with active engagement in society and in an economy which recognises the opportunities offered by the UK's changing age profile.

So which government department should have responded to the report? Maybe it should have been the Department of Health, given the many issues to its address. Maybe it should have been the Department of Trade and Industry, given the economic opportunities missed by our culture's focus on youth. Perhaps it should have been the Office of Science and Innovation, as it now is, embedded in the DTI, and perhaps especially the research councils, given some of the potentially cost-saving and life-improving opportunities which research suggests.
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC310
 

But what happened? The Government appear to have seen the word "ageing" and reflectively said, "Oh, pest—oldies. Send this report about oldies to the pensions people". And the DWP, which perhaps understandably sometimes seems to see old people as pension-consuming burdens and which has not yet been transmogrified by its excellent new Minister, has issued a basically dismissive response, essentially failing to grapple with many of the recommendations.

I shall give four specific examples. The first, touched on by my noble friend Lord Sutherland, involves stroke. As noble Lords heard, one specific recommendation concerns CT scans for stroke victims. The need to carry them out quickly is pre-eminent, and yet we heard that 22 to 30 per cent of people—the figure varies—get them within one day, much less one hour, which is way behind many other countries and the Netherlands in particular. That is not for lack of equipment but for lack of staffing and lack of protocols. Delays in scanning, where "time lost is brain lost", are not bad just for the victims but they end up causing greater costs to the NHS. Yet the Select Committee's direct recommendations on the subject are essentially dismissed and, instead, we are offered some obfuscatory bureaucratic waffle.

The second example is healthy life expectancy. We were told that healthy life expectancy is increasing more slowly than life expectancy itself so that most older people can expect more years of ill-health. If that is true, it is really worrying. But other experts emphasised that the problem with the data arises not least because much of the data is self-assessment and it is very hard to control with rising expectations. We recommended further study of this important issue, but one of the more careless paragraphs in the Government's response again brushed that aside, reasserting the gap between healthy life expectancy and life expectancy, which frankly is in real danger of becoming one of those "truths" established by repeated assertion rather than examination of the facts.

The third example concerns the Economic and Social Research Council and research on ageing. Leading on from the previous point, we asked the ESRC what proportion of its budget was spent on topics related to ageing and we were told that 1.2 per cent of the economic and social research is devoted to such topics. Significantly, that is a smaller amount than is the case with any other research council, including the Engineering and Physical Sciences Research Council, which one would not think of as a major spender in this area. We found that astonishing. At the very least, the ESRC could be asking why people report more years of ill health and whether that reflects a reality or a Zeitgeist. Much more importantly, the ESRC might well be reflecting on a metalevel study in which it examines the intellectual fads and fashions that drive its agenda, resulting in the major social and economic consequences of the ageing population being deemed so uninteresting.

Finally, a much wider concern, about which we have heard already, is the many and varied ways in which "market forces", contrary to one of our primary
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column GC311
 
established religious beliefs, appear to neglect the economic opportunities offered by products oriented to older people. I refer to assistive technologies, IT products with instructions which assume that the user did not spent his or her youth playing computer games and which ideally would be written in a less non-linear language—that is, not the kind of language where you have to understand all of it before you understand any of it—and a variety of others, such as those explained to us by my noble friend Lady Finlay and touched on by other speakers.

Incidentally, one of my favourite factoids from the Institute of Practitioners in Advertising was that 39 per cent of UK marketing directors are under the age of 35 and only 10 per cent are over the age of 50. From the youth-oriented media and the advertisements therein, one would have difficulty in guessing that in the UK people over the age of 50 own 80 per cent of the UK's assets, 60 per cent of its savings and, while some of those are a bit inflexible, 40 per cent of its disposable income. More deliberate emphasis on products useful and/or tempting to this large segment of the economy would seem to be sensible on purely economic, apart from trade and industry, grounds, never mind the large and more important issues of quality of life.

I am not sure that I agree that a dedicated institute, staffed by some more well intentioned and hard-working civil servants, is either a necessary or a sufficient answer. But I most earnestly hope—and I expect the excellent new Minister at the DWP to say—that the Government will have second thoughts about its rather dismissive initial response to our report.

    "What is less well understood is that poor nutrition also contributes to an acceleration of features of the underlying ageing process, leading to an increased risk of diabetes, cardiovascular disease and stroke in later life.     the case of poor nutrition, beneficially in the case of good nutrition), the links between healthy eating and healthy ageing need to be better understood and communicated to the public".

5 Jun 2006 : Column GC317
 
although if it were borne out in practice, it would be a matter of great concern. Alas, the report gives no specific objective measure. In chapter 3, we find the statement, "Ageing begins at birth". That is certainly true, but the expectation of ageing surely arises very much later, let us say in one's 40s or 50s, when the body starts to slow down and the mind controlling it becomes less positive. I would like to have seen a graph for the decline in exercise in the 50-plus age group. As the Royal College of Physicians in Edinburgh told the committee, physical activity is the major modifiable influence on health in old age. It also said, surprisingly to me, that lost fitness can be regained with regular activity, even in extreme old age. I was also interested to learn, if I understood the report correctly, that a three-month exercise programme can rejuvenate muscle mass by a 15-year equivalent at whatever age it is undertaken. So the message is keep active, but that applies not only to the body. Exercising, or rather the will to exercise, is a state of mind. If it is boring, you will be less inclined to take it. This applies also when you are ill or, perhaps more importantly, feel ill. Therefore, exercising the mind is vital, and, for a long quality of life, essential.

The body clearly needs nutrition, and good nutrition at that. I agree that the provision of food content labelling is a welcome change, and the Department of Health's White Paper, Choosing Health, encourages this. I am no doctor—although I have spent a little time in what one might call "the health peripheries"—but if saturated fats, for example, are bad, and fruit and vegetables are good, that must apply throughout life.

I accept that circulatory disease, respiratory disease and cancer are the three most likely causes of death in those who are over 65. the breast, colon, lung, pancreas and so on. The noble Baronesses, Lady Murphy and Lady Finlay, will correct me if I am wrong, but what difference does age make to those?

Written Statements

5 Jun 2006 : Column WS65
 

Armed Forces: Voting

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Ministry of Defence (Lord Drayson): My honourable friend the Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State for Defence (Mr Tom Watson) has made. the following Written Ministerial Statement.

I have today placed in the Library of the House a copy of the report of the survey on service voting conducted by the Defence Analytical Services Agency.

The Ministry of Defence has been working with the Electoral Commission to improve the electoral process for the Armed Forces. This survey was undertaken to provide hard evidence of the numbers of service personnel who register and cast their vote. It was also designed to provide information to help us judge how best to continue to encourage service personnel to do so in future.

I welcome the survey, which gives us hard facts to target future work. The survey indicates that the services are broadly consistent with national trends on voting in the general election, given that they demographically fall within the lower age ranges where fewer people voted. In addition it shows that 60 per cent of service personnel are currently registered, with a significant majority of those (67 per cent) choosing to register as ordinary rather than service voters.

Although these figures indicate that anecdotal evidence of a very significant drop in numbers registered was pessimistic, they do show that there is still work to be done. The survey suggests, for example, that we have been less effective in getting the message across to those serving overseas and other ranks. We are working closely with the Electoral Commission to analyse the survey, to understand the reasons behind the results, and to use them to make further improvements to the quality of information available to all service personnel.

We remain committed to improving arrangements for the service community to exercise their right to vote, and I am keen to improve further the quality and timeliness of information on registration and voting options and procedures and to simplify them where possible. We will continue to work hard, with the Electoral Commission, to achieve this.

EU: Education Council

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Education and Skills (Lord Adonis): My honourable friend the Minister of State for Lifelong Learning, Further and Higher Education (Bill Rammell) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column WS66
 

The Scottish Executive Minister for Education and Young People (Mr Peter Peacock) and Ms Anne Lambert, UK Deputy Permanent Representative to the EU, represented the UK during the Education Council in Brussels.

At the Education Council:

Ministers agreed conclusions on the European Indicator of Language Competence. The agreement covered the basic parameters of the indicator, including level of testing and the languages to be included, as requested by the European Council in 2002. An advisory board of member states' experts will work out the technical specifications. A sentence was added to make clear that the advisory board should take into consideration the need to prevent undue administrative and financial burdens on member states.

The council agreed that pupils should be tested in two foreign languages at the end of ISCED level II (ages 9–14 in the UK). However, in those member states where only one foreign language was taught at that level, testing of the second language would be at ISCED III (15–19 and therefore after the end of compulsory education in the UK). Some member states would have preferred all testing to take place at ISCED III, but could accept the compromise.

The council agreed a general approach for a recommendation of the European Parliament and of the Council on Key Competences for Lifelong Learning. The presidency expected to reach early agreement with the European Parliament. The Commission (Figel) said the recommendation could be finally adopted at the Education Council in November 2006.

The UK maintained its parliamentary scrutiny reserve, noting that the UK Parliament thought the text went too far in the direction of defining and prescribing the content of curricula. The parliamentary scrutiny committees had particular difficulty with the idea that knowledge about European integration and structures was "essential". The UK asked the council to replace "essential" with "desirable" or "important". The presidency noted the UK's concerns and outstanding scrutiny reserve but declined to alter the text.

The council also agreed a general approach for the recommendation of the European Parliament and of the Council on a Quality Charter for Mobility. The presidency said a joint report of the European Parliament's Employment and Culture Committees was expected in September.

Ministers exchanged views on the financial aspects of the Lifelong Learning Programme. The Commission said that, following the agreement on the EU financial perspective (2007–13), the total budget for the programme would be €6.97 billion. A formal proposal would be adopted on 24 May. It would break down the funding between the four main sub-programmes as follows: 13 per cent to Comenius (schools); 40 per cent to Erasmus (higher education); 25 per cent to Leonardo da Vinci (vocational training); and 3 per cent to Grundtvig (adult learning).
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column WS67
 

In a full table round, several member states regretted the substantial reduction in the budget originally proposed by the Commission. However, most welcomed the increased flexibility and decentralisation, which would allow unused funding to be transferred more easily between sub-programmes.

Many member states could accept the distribution of funding proposed by the Commission in order to ensure that the new programme started on time. None the less, most argued that priority should be given to one or other of the sub-programmes. The UK suggested that Grundtvig should receive 6 per cent of the overall budget, and said the increase should come from the much larger Erasmus programme. It was important for the council to send a signal about the importance of lifelong learning and the need to give greater assistance to older and low-skilled workers, particularly given the demographic challenges facing the EU. A number of other member states also asked for Grundtvig to receive additional funding. Several member states preferred to give priority to Erasmus, while a few others favoured Leonardo da Vinci. The attention of the council was also drawn to the European Council's request to double the number of mobility grants under these programmes between 2006 and 2013.

Summing up, the Commission said that there would be some room for flexibility, but reminded Ministers that the European Council had highlighted the particular importance of Erasmus and Leonardo da Vinci. The Commission urged member states to consider how they would use alternative sources of funding, including the EU structural funds. The Erasmus Mundus programme, which could no longer be funded from the new lifelong learning programme, would continue until 2008. The Commission would at that point make a proposal for the future funding of that programme.

In a debate on the contribution of education to the EU Sustainable Development Strategy, Ministers agreed the central role and importance of education and lifelong learning. Most stressed the importance of establishing strong links between policies for sustainable development, education, citizenship and cohesion. Member states, including the UK, generally did not want to fix new EU objectives or targets. Existing processes, notably the Lisbon strategy and the education and training 2010 work programme, were sufficient. One member state also stressed the need for synergy with the UN's 10-year strategy for sustainable development.

Under "any other business", the Commission presented its recent communication on higher education. The UK stressed the need for a concrete follow-up of the communication and encouraged Ministers to have a full discussion in the near future about the issues it raised. The UK informed the council about the "peer learning" seminar it would host in October on the theme of modernising universities. Immigration: National Insurance Numbers

The Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State, Department for Work and Pensions (Lord Hunt of Kings Heath): My honourable friend the Parliamentary Under-Secretary of State (James Plaskitt) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

From July 2006, the Department for Work and Pensions will introduce a "right to work" condition into Jobcentre Plus' national insurance numbers (NINOs) allocation and decision-making process for employment-related applications. Regulation 9 of the Social Security (Credits & Treatment of Contributions & National Insurance Numbers) Regulation 2001 will be amended to enable this change.

Any individual applying for a NINO in connection with employment who does not have the right to work here legally will be refused one. This change follows a review conducted by the department into the existing legislation governing the allocation of NINOs.

Previous regulations since the 1948 Act was introduced establishing the national insurance system have never included a "right to work" condition. NINOs were never designed to act as evidence of an individual's identity or to confirm that people have the right to live or work in this country. Current legislation requires employers to check that an individual has the right to work under Section 8 of the 1996 Asylum & Immigration Act.

This Statement gives details of progress to date.

5 Jun 2006 : Column WS69
 
between January and April 2006, which represents an increase of 108 per cent over the corresponding period in 2005.

A significant amount of work continues to promote voluntary returns, and there is to be a high level of interest to take up the scheme. Only those who applied for asylum before 1 January 2006 can benefit from the enhanced package of reintegration assistance, and they must leave the UK between 1 July and 31 December 2006 in order to qualify.

Indonesia Earthquake

The Lord President of the Council (Baroness Amos): My right honourable friend the Secretary of State for International Development (Hilary Benn) has made the following Written Ministerial Statement.

On the morning of 27 May at 5.54, Indonesians living on the island of Java suffered an earthquake measuring 6.3 on the Richter scale. The epicentre was near the southern coast of the island, approximately 37 kilometres south of Yogyakarta.

Initial estimates by the Government of Indonesia are that some 6,000 people were killed. More than 45,000 people have been injured and some 140,000 homes have been damaged or destroyed, leaving at least 200,000 people displaced.

DfID's humanitarian response team was immediately made aware of the earthquake and began collecting information and assessing the need for assistance. The Government of Indonesia confirmed that they were taking responsibility for the national response and overall co-ordination and they did not appeal for international assistance. However they made it clear that offers of assistance would be welcome. I immediately announced £1 million in support of the International Federation for the Red Cross and Red Crescent Societies and £3 million in support of the United Nations agencies. UK search and rescue teams were put on standby but were not needed, with search and rescue being carried out by local survivors and Indonesian rescue teams. A two-person assessment team from DfID deployed to Indonesia on 28 May and based themselves in Yogyakarta from where they carried out assessments and met partner organisations. The team is now in Jakarta and will return to London by the end of this week.

On Friday 2 June, I announced another £1 million to go to NGOs working on the ground in Java, bringing DfID's total commitment to the relief effort to £5 million. This will contribute to health and shelter, and help people to restore their livelihoods. We are also contributing some €600,000 as the UK's share of €3 million from the EC.
 
5 Jun 2006 : Column WS70
 

The Indonesian and international relief effort is well underway. The Indonesian Government have pledged $115 million, including $10 million for emergency response and $105 million for rehabilitation and reconstruction. The UN issued its emergency response plan on 2 June, detailing its planned activities for the next six months.

With the support of DfID and other donors, national and international relief bodies working in Java are able to cope with the relief needs. As the need for longer term rehabilitation and reconstruction funds becomes clearer, DfID will be looking to see where it can provide further support. We will also continue to monitor volcanic activity on Mount Merapi, which was of concern prior to the earthquake.

Further information on the emergency response can be found on DfID's website at www.dfid.gov.uk.
